[Subsystem Purpose and Boundaries](#1-subsystem-purpose-and-boundaries)\n2. [Key Components and Responsibility Split](#2-key-components-and-responsibility-split)\n3. [End-to-End Request Flow](#3-end-to-end-request-flow)\n4. [The Watch Cache and Storage Layer](#4-the-watch-cache-and-storage-layer)\n5. [Extension Points and Customization Hooks](#5-extension-points-and-customization-hooks)\n6. [Failure Modes, Limits, and Design Tradeoffs](#6-failure-modes-limits-and-design-tradeoffs)\n7. [Operational Implications](#7-operational-implications)\n8. [File and Directory Map](#8-file-and-directory-map)\n\n---\n\n## 1. Subsystem Purpose and Boundaries\n\nThe `k8s.io/apiserver` library (`staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/`) is the shared framework from which every Kubernetes API server is built. It is not a standalone binary; it is a Go library providing the scaffolding that the three concrete servers composing `kube-apiserver`, as well as any extension API server built with the aggregation pattern, embed directly.\n\nIts responsibilities span the full lifetime of an API request:\n- Accepting and terminating TLS connections.\n- Authenticating callers and authorizing their intent.\n- Throttling and prioritizing traffic with API Priority and Fairness.\n- Dispatching requests to per-resource REST handlers.\n- Coordinating admission control (mutation and validation).\n- Persisting state to and watching from etcd, with an in-process watch cache as an intermediary.\n- Emitting audit events and serving observability endpoints.\n\n**Upstream dependencies** the library consumes:\n- `k8s.io/apimachinery` — the `runtime.Scheme`, `GroupVersionKind`, serialization codecs, and object lifecycle types.\n- `k8s.io/client-go` — informers and REST clients (primarily used within the admission subsystem and post-start hooks).\n- `go.etcd.io/etcd/client/v3` — the etcd client, accessed only through the `pkg/storage/etcd3` package.\n- `k8s.io/kube-openapi` — the OpenAPI v2/v3 spec builder.\n\n**Downstream consumers**:\n- `cmd/kube-apiserver` — the main Kubernetes control plane API server.\n- `staging/src/k8s.io/kube-aggregator` — the aggregation layer that delegates to extension API servers.\n- `staging/src/k8s.io/apiextensions-apiserver` — the CRD handler.\n- Any extension API server built with `RecommendedOptions` (the `sample-apiserver` pattern).\n\nThe library deliberately has no knowledge of any specific resource type (Pod, Deployment, etc.). Those are registered at startup by the consuming binary through the `APIGroupInfo` and `runtime.Scheme` mechanisms.\n\n---\n\n## 2. Key Components and Responsibility Split\n\n### 2.1 Configuration and Bootstrap (`pkg/server/`)\n\nConfiguration flows through a layered struct hierarchy before any server starts:\n\n**`RecommendedOptions`** (`pkg/server/options/recommended.go`): A convenience aggregate of all the option structs an extension API server typically needs — etcd, TLS serving, authentication, authorization, audit, admission, egress, and tracing. Each field maps 1-to-1 to a domain-specific options struct (e.g., `EtcdOptions`, `AuditOptions`). When `ApplyTo` is called, each option struct populates the corresponding section of the `Config`.\n\n**`Config`** (`pkg/server/config.go:112`): The central struct that captures all runtime configuration for a `GenericAPIServer`. Notable fields:\n- `Authentication AuthenticationInfo` and `Authorization AuthorizationInfo` — pluggable auth.\n- `AdmissionControl admission.Interface` — the assembled admission chain.\n- `FlowControl utilflowcontrol.Interface` — API Priority and Fairness; if nil, falls back to the simpler `MaxRequestsInFlight` semaphore.\n- `BuildHandlerChainFunc` — a replaceable hook that constructs the HTTP middleware stack. Defaults to `DefaultBuildHandlerChain`.\n- `RESTOptionsGetter genericregistry.RESTOptionsGetter` — factory for per-resource storage backend configuration.\n- `ResourceTransformers storagevalue.ResourceTransformers` — the encryption-at-rest transformer chain.\n\n**`GenericAPIServer`** (`pkg/server/genericapiserver.go:109`): The server runtime object. It holds the HTTP handler (`Handler *APIServerHandler`), the delegation target for the next server in the chain (`delegationTarget DelegationTarget`), lifecycle signals, health check registries, and the audit backend. After all API groups are installed, `PrepareRun()` finalizes health endpoints and OpenAPI spec installation, and `RunWithContext()` starts the TLS listener.\n\n### 2.2 Server Delegation Chain\n\nThe `kube-apiserver` binary is not a single server but a **chain of three `GenericAPIServer` instances** constructed in `cmd/kube-apiserver/app/server.go:CreateServerChain` (line 176):\n\n```\nCreateServerChain():\n  apiExtensionsServer = config.ApiExtensions.New(emptyDelegate)    // innermost\n  kubeAPIServer       = config.KubeAPIs.New(apiExtensionsServer)\n  aggregatorServer    = CreateAggregatorServer(kubeAPIServer, ...)  // outermost, receives all traffic\n```\n\nEach server implements `DelegationTarget` (`pkg/server/genericapiserver.go:308`). When a request arrives at the outermost server (the aggregator) and no `APIService` claims the path, the request is forwarded to the next delegate's `UnprotectedHandler()`. This continues until a server handles the request or the innermost `emptyDelegate` returns 404. The delegation is composable: post-start hooks, health checks, and lifecycle signals from all three servers are merged by the outermost server at `PrepareRun()` time.\n\n### 2.3 HTTP Handler Chain (`pkg/server/filters/`, `pkg/endpoints/filters/`)\n\nThe HTTP middleware stack is assembled inside-out by `DefaultBuildHandlerChain` (`pkg/server/config.go:1015`). Because Go's net/http middleware wraps from outside in, the order of `handler = WithX(handler, ...)` calls is the reverse of request-arrival order. Reading the function bottom-to-top gives the actual order a request traverses:\n\n1. `WithAuditInit` — allocates the audit event for the request.\n2. `WithPanicRecovery` — catches panics and returns a 500.\n3. `WithMuxAndDiscoveryComplete` — blocks requests with 503 until all routes are installed (prevents spurious 404s at startup).\n4. `WithRequestReceivedTimestamp` — stamps the request start time.\n5. `WithRequestInfo` — parses the URL into a `RequestInfo` struct (resource, namespace, name, verb) used by auth.\n6. `WithRoutine` (optional, feature-gated) — dispatches downstream handlers to a goroutine to reduce stack depth.\n7. `WithLatencyTrackers`, `WithHTTPLogging`, `WithHSTS`, `WithCacheControl` — observability and HTTP hygiene.\n8. `WithRetryAfter` (optional) — during shutdown, returns 429 with `Retry-After` instead of accepting new work.\n9. `WithWaitGroup` / `WithWatchTerminationDuringShutdown` — drain tracking for graceful shutdown.\n10. `WithRequestDeadline` — enforces overall request timeout from config.\n11. `WithTimeoutForNonLongRunningRequests` — runs the downstream handler in a goroutine with a deadline.\n12. `WithWarningRecorder` — collects deprecation warnings to emit as response headers.\n13. `WithCORS` — sets CORS headers.\n14. `WithAuthentication` — calls the union authenticator; on failure routes to `failedHandler`.\n15. `WithTracing` (optional, feature-gated) — starts an OpenTelemetry span after authentication so the caller's identity is available.\n16. `WithAudit` — logs the audit event at the `RequestReceived` stage.\n17. `WithImpersonation` / `WithConstrainedImpersonation` — allows callers with permission to act as another identity.\n18. `WithPriorityAndFairness` (or `WithMaxInFlightLimit`) — classifies and queues the request.\n19. `WithAuthorization` — checks if the authenticated identity is permitted to perform the requested verb on the resource.\n\nThe final destination after this chain is the `APIServerHandler`, which routes to go-restful (for API resources) or the non-restful mux (for `/metrics`, `/healthz`, `/openapi`, etc.).\n\n### 2.4 API Group Registration and `APIInstaller` (`pkg/endpoints/`)\n\nServing a resource type requires two steps: defining storage and registering it.\n\n**`APIGroupInfo`** (`pkg/server/genericapiserver.go:71`): A bundle that describes one API group:\n- `PrioritizedVersions []schema.GroupVersion` — ordered list of versions; the first is the preferred version advertised to clients.\n- `VersionedResourcesStorageMap map[string]map[string]rest.Storage` — maps `version → resource name → REST storage implementation`.\n- `Scheme *runtime.Scheme` — the type registry and conversion hub for this group.\n- `NegotiatedSerializer runtime.NegotiatedSerializer` — controls content-type negotiation (JSON, protobuf, CBOR).\n\n`GenericAPIServer.InstallAPIGroups()` (line 876) calls `installAPIResources`, which creates an `APIGroupVersion` per version, then calls `APIInstaller.Install()` (`pkg/endpoints/installer.go`). The installer iterates the `VersionedResourcesStorageMap` and, for each resource, performs interface-based capability detection on the `rest.Storage` implementation. If the storage also implements `rest.Getter`, a GET handler is registered. If it implements `rest.Creater`, a POST handler is registered, and so on. This pattern means that the set of HTTP verbs exposed for a resource is determined entirely by which `rest.*` interfaces its storage object implements.\n\n### 2.5 `genericregistry.Store` (`pkg/registry/generic/registry/store.go`)\n\n`Store` is the canonical implementation of `rest.StandardStorage`. Extension API servers and all built-in Kubernetes resources embed it. It holds:\n\n- `CreateStrategy rest.RESTCreateStrategy`, `UpdateStrategy rest.RESTUpdateStrategy`, `DeleteStrategy rest.RESTDeleteStrategy` — the **Strategy** pattern: resource-specific business logic (validation, defaulting, name generation, garbage collection handling) separated from generic CRUD mechanics.\n- `BeginCreate BeginCreateFunc`, `BeginUpdate BeginUpdateFunc` — optional hooks that return a commit/rollback function, executed around the storage write (line 66–75). Almost never needed; the comment in the source recommends against their use.\n- `AfterCreate AfterCreateFunc`, `AfterUpdate AfterUpdateFunc`, `AfterDelete AfterDeleteFunc` — post-write hooks that run before the decorator.\n- `Decorator func(runtime.Object)` — applies non-stored transformations to objects returned from the store (line 170). Not watched.\n- `Storage DryRunnableStorage` — wraps the underlying `storage.Interface`, intercepting dry-run requests.\n- `PredicateFunc` — resource-specific label/field filter, used to push down list/watch predicates toward storage.\n\n### 2.6 Storage Layer (`pkg/storage/`)\n\n**`storage.Interface`** (`pkg/storage/interfaces.go:169`): The abstract storage contract. Operations: `Create`, `Delete`, `Watch`, `Get`, `GetList`, `GuaranteedUpdate`. The `GuaranteedUpdate` method accepts an `UpdateFunc` that is retried on conflict, implementing the optimistic-concurrency write loop inside the storage layer.\n\n**`etcd3.store`** (`pkg/storage/etcd3/store.go`): The only production implementation of `storage.Interface`. It speaks to etcd via the `clientv3` library and the Kubernetes-specific `go.etcd.io/etcd/client/v3/kubernetes` variant. Each object is serialized (and optionally encrypted via `value.Transformer`) before being written. The `resourceVersion` of an object is the etcd `mod_revision` of the key cast to a string — this is why `resourceVersion` is globally ordered across all resource types.\n\n**`Cacher`** (`pkg/storage/cacher/cacher.go`): A `storage.Interface` wrapper that interposes an in-memory reflector between the etcd client and callers. Initialized with a `storage.Interface` and a `Config` (line 80), it performs a LIST to snapshot state, then opens a persistent WATCH from that `resourceVersion`. Most `Get`, `GetList`, and `Watch` calls are served from the in-memory store. Bookmark events (sent at `defaultBookmarkFrequency = 1 minute`) advance the cacher's known `resourceVersion` even when objects are idle, preventing expensive resyncs.\n\n### 2.7 Admission Control (`pkg/admission/`)\n\nThe admission system is a chain of plugins ordered by the `Plugins` registry. The chain type (`chainAdmissionHandler`, `pkg/admission/chain.go:23`) implements two separate passes:\n\n- **`Admit`** (mutation) — calls every plugin that implements `MutationInterface` and handles the request operation. First error aborts the chain.\n- **`Validate`** (validation) — calls every plugin that implements `ValidationInterface`. First error aborts.\n\nBoth passes share the `Attributes` interface (`pkg/admission/interfaces.go:31`), which exposes the deserialized object (`GetObject()`), the old object (`GetOldObject()` for updates), the user, the GVK, the operation, and a dry-run flag. Importantly, the object is deserialized and converted to the internal hub version before entering admission, so plugins always see the internal representation regardless of which wire version the client sent.\n\nWebhook-based plugins (`MutatingWebhookConfiguration`, `ValidatingWebhookConfiguration`) and CEL-based policy plugins (`ValidatingAdmissionPolicy`, `MutatingAdmissionPolicy`) are layered on top of this same `MutationInterface`/`ValidationInterface` contract via the `pkg/admission/plugin/policy/generic/Plugin` struct.\n\n### 2.8 Audit (`pkg/audit/`)\n\nThe audit subsystem is policy-driven. An `AuditPolicyRuleEvaluator` (configured via `pkg/audit/policy/checker.go`) matches each request against an ordered rule list, producing an audit `Level` (None, Metadata, Request, RequestResponse) and the stages to log (`RequestReceived`, `ResponseStarted`, `ResponseComplete`, `Panic`). The `WithAudit` filter in the handler chain populates an `auditinternal.Event` object stored in the request context; individual stages are written to the `AuditBackend` (log file or webhook) as they complete.\n\n---\n\n## 3. End-to-End Request Flow\n\nThe following traces a `kubectl apply` (a server-side apply PATCH) against a Deployment through the complete system.\n\n### 3.1 Network Ingress to Handler Chain\n\nThe TLS listener (`pkg/server/secure_serving.go`) accepts the TCP connection and hands it to `net/http`'s `ServeTLS`. The Go HTTP/2 or HTTP/1.1 framer reads the request and calls the top of the handler chain assembled by `DefaultBuildHandlerChain`.\n\nAt this point the request body has not been read. The filters at the top of the chain (panic recovery, request info, timeout framing) operate on the URL and headers alone.\n\n### 3.2 Authentication\n\n`WithAuthentication` calls the **union authenticator**, which tries each registered authenticator in sequence — client-certificate (mTLS), bearer token (static or OIDC), service account JWT, request-header (for the aggregation proxy). The first to return a non-anonymous `user.Info` wins. If none succeed, `failedHandler` returns 401 and writes an audit event.\n\nThe resolved `user.Info` is stored in the request context via `request.WithUser`.\n\n### 3.3 API Priority and Fairness\n\n`WithPriorityAndFairness` (`pkg/server/filters/`) classifies the request by matching it against the cluster's `FlowSchema` objects. The matching produces a `PriorityLevel` and a `FlowDistinguisher` (e.g., user or namespace). The request is enqueued in the appropriate virtual queue. A work estimator (`flowcontrolrequest.NewWorkEstimator`) assigns a seat-count (width) and duration estimate to the request before queuing. If no seats are available, the request waits up to its deadline. If the queue is too long, the request is rejected with 429.\n\n### 3.4 Authorization\n\n`WithAuthorization` calls the **union authorizer** (RBAC, Node, Webhook, etc.). It passes a `DecisionRecord` containing the verb derived from the request method + URL and the resource parsed by `RequestInfoResolver`. The first authorizer to return `Allow` or `Deny` wins; `NoOpinion` passes to the next. A denial produces a 403.\n\n### 3.5 Routing and Deserialization\n\nThe request reaches the go-restful `Container`, which matches the URL pattern (e.g., `/apis/apps/v1/namespaces/{namespace}/deployments/{name}`) to the handler registered by `APIInstaller`. For a PATCH request, this is the handler returned by `restfulPatchResource`.\n\nInside the handler (`pkg/endpoints/handlers/patch.go`), the request body is now read (`limitedReadBodyWithRecordMetric`) and deserialized. Content-type negotiation selects the appropriate decoder (JSON, protobuf, CBOR, or `application/apply-patch+yaml` for server-side apply). The object is decoded into the internal hub version via the `NegotiatedSerializer` and then converted using the `Scheme`.\n\n### 3.6 Admission: Mutation Pass\n\nThe handler constructs an `admission.Attributes` record and calls `admit.Admit(ctx, admissionAttributes, scope)`. The chain iterates all `MutationInterface` plugins in order. For server-side apply, the `FieldManager` (`pkg/endpoints/handlers/fieldmanager/`) updates `managedFields` metadata to track field ownership. Webhook-based mutators may inject new fields; the reinvocation policy in `pkg/admission/reinvocation.go` may call mutation plugins a second time if any plugin indicated a change.\n\n### 3.7 Admission: Validation Pass\n\nAfter mutation is complete, `admit.Validate(ctx, admissionAttributes, scope)` is called. Each `ValidationInterface` plugin runs in sequence. CEL-based `ValidatingAdmissionPolicy` plugins compiled at policy-creation time evaluate their expressions against the (now-mutated) object. A validation failure returns a 422 Unprocessable Entity.\n\nThe resource's own `Strategy.Validate()` (inside `genericregistry.Store.Update`) provides a final, strategy-level validation pass that runs after admission.\n\n### 3.8 Storage Write\n\n`genericregistry.Store.Update` calls `Storage.GuaranteedUpdate`, which (via the `DryRunnableStorage` wrapper) delegates to `etcd3.store.GuaranteedUpdate`. The etcd store:\n\n1. Reads the current value of the key with a `GET`.\n2. Calls the `UpdateFunc` provided by the registry, which runs strategy-level precondition checks (`PrepareForUpdate`, `ValidateUpdate`).\n3. Issues an etcd `txn` (compare-and-swap): if the current `mod_revision` matches the revision from step 1, write the new value. If not, retry from step 1.\n\nThe `resourceVersion` written into the returned object is the new `mod_revision` from etcd, converted to a string.\n\n### 3.9 Watch Cache Propagation and Response\n\nThe etcd WATCH held by the `Cacher` sees the new revision event. It decodes the updated object, applies it to the in-memory store, and fans out the event to all registered `watchChan` subscribers (controllers, `kubectl --watch`, etc.).\n\nBack in the request goroutine, the response is serialized to the negotiated content-type and written to the HTTP response. The audit `ResponseComplete` event is flushed to the audit backend.\n\n---\n\n## 4. The Watch Cache and Storage Layer\n\n### 4.1 Cacher Initialization and Consistency\n\nWhen the `Cacher` starts (`pkg/storage/cacher/cacher.go`), it performs a paginated `GetList` against etcd using `storageWatchListPageSize = 10000` objects per page (line 65). After the list completes at revision R, it opens a `WATCH` from R+1. All cache state is captured at revision R, and subsequent events flow in order from R+1 onwards.\n\nFor **consistent reads** (when a client passes a `resourceVersion` of `\"\"` or requests an exact version), the cacher first fetches the latest etcd revision number without reading data (a cheap etcd linearizable read), then blocks until its internal `resourceVersion` catches up to that revision before serving from cache.\n\n### 4.2 Watch Fanout\n\nEach `Watch` call against the cacher returns a `watchChan` that receives a filtered stream of events. Events are dispatched to all registered watchers from a single goroutine. The `EventFreshDuration` (default ~75 seconds, line 72: `DefaultEventFreshDuration = defaultBookmarkFrequency + 15*time.Second`) defines the minimum event history window kept in memory. A client that reconnects within this window can resume without a full relist.\n\n### 4.3 Encryption at Rest\n\nBefore bytes are written to etcd, they pass through the `value.Transformer` chain (`pkg/storage/value/`). The transformer is configured by the `EncryptionConfiguration` resource and can chain multiple providers: identity (no-op), secretbox (AES-256), KMS v1 (AES-CBC via gRPC), and KMS v2 (AES-GCM via gRPC, `pkg/storage/value/encrypt/envelope/kmsv2/`). Reads decode by trying each provider in order; writes use the first (current) provider. This allows live key rotation without downtime.\n\n---\n\n## 5. Extension Points and Customization Hooks\n\n### 5.1 Building a New API Server with `RecommendedOptions`\n\nThe standard pattern for a new extension API server is to create a `RecommendedOptions` struct (`pkg/server/options/recommended.go:34`), call `ApplyTo` to populate a `server.RecommendedConfig`, then construct a `GenericAPIServer` and install custom `APIGroupInfo` structs. The `sample-apiserver` (`staging/src/k8s.io/sample-apiserver/`) demonstrates this pattern end-to-end.\n\nRisk: Extension API servers share the same admission and auth configuration as the core server when accessed through the aggregation layer. If an extension server does not properly validate its own admission, the aggregation proxy will forward unauthenticated requests (it authenticates using the request-header credential).\n\n### 5.2 Custom `Strategy` Objects\n\nTo add a built-in resource (not accessible without modifying the source), implement:\n- `rest.RESTCreateStrategy` — `PrepareForCreate(ctx, obj)`, `Validate(ctx, obj) field.ErrorList`, `Canonicalize(obj)`.\n- `rest.RESTUpdateStrategy` — `PrepareForUpdate(ctx, obj, old)`, `ValidateUpdate(ctx, obj, old) field.ErrorList`.\n- `rest.RESTDeleteStrategy` — typically embedded from a default implementation.\n\nEmbed these in a `genericregistry.Store` and install via `InstallAPIGroups`. The strategy objects are the correct place for business logic that must be enforced even on in-cluster admin actions — unlike webhooks, they cannot be bypassed.\n\n### 5.3 Custom Admission Plugins\n\nRegister a plugin with `admission.RegisterPlugin(name, factory)` in an `init()` function. The factory receives an `io.Reader` for per-plugin configuration. The plugin struct must implement at minimum `admission.Interface` (to report which operations it handles) and one of `MutationInterface` or `ValidationInterface`.\n\nTo access cluster state (e.g., to look up a Namespace during admission), implement the `initializer.WantsInformerFactory` or `initializer.WantsExternalKubeClientSet` interfaces. The `admission.PluginInitializer` mechanism (`pkg/admission/initializer/`) injects these dependencies after plugin construction. This is why admission plugins must not call the API server synchronously during `Admit`/`Validate` — they should read from their informer caches to avoid re-entrant requests.\n\nRisk: A misbehaving admission webhook or plugin that times out degrades every write to the affected resource. The `FailurePolicy` field on `ValidatingWebhookConfiguration` controls whether a timeout causes a hard failure (`Fail`) or silently passes (`Ignore`). Choosing `Ignore` for security-sensitive policies is dangerous; choosing `Fail` creates an availability dependency on the webhook endpoint.\n\n### 5.4 Custom Handler Chain (`BuildHandlerChainFunc`)\n\n`Config.BuildHandlerChainFunc` (line 186 in `pkg/server/config.go`) is a replaceable function. Replacing it entirely is almost never correct. The safe pattern is:\n\n```go\nconfig.BuildHandlerChainFunc = func(handler http.Handler, c *server.Config) http.Handler {\n    handler = myCustomMiddleware(handler)\n    return server.DefaultBuildHandlerChain(handler, c)\n}\n```\n\nInserting middleware _before_ calling `DefaultBuildHandlerChain` places it after all standard filters (inside the chain, close to the handler). Wrapping the result of `DefaultBuildHandlerChain` places it before all filters (outside the chain, first to see raw requests).\n\nRisk: Custom middleware placed outside authentication can observe unauthenticated requests, including attacker-controlled data. Custom middleware placed inside authentication has access to `user.Info` from the context but runs after the request has already been admitted by the concurrency limiter.\n\n### 5.5 Post-Start and Pre-Shutdown Hooks\n\n`GenericAPIServer.AddPostStartHook(name, fn)` registers a `PostStartHookFunc` (`pkg/server/hooks.go:43`). Each hook runs in its own goroutine after the TLS listener is open. Hooks receive a `PostStartHookContext` containing a `LoopbackClientConfig` (privileged, certificate-authenticated connection to the local API server). This is the canonical way to run initialization that requires the server to be accepting requests (e.g., installing default `FlowSchema` objects, setting up CRD indices).\n\n`AddPreShutdownHook` registers a `PreShutdownHookFunc` that runs before the shutdown delay period begins, allowing in-process controllers to quiesce before the server stops accepting traffic.\n\nRisk: A post-start hook that never returns will block `readyz` from succeeding, making the server appear not-ready indefinitely. The `LivezGracePeriod` (`Config` field) provides a window during which `/livez` returns true even if post-start hooks have not completed, preventing premature process restart.\n\n### 5.6 Custom Storage Backends\n\nReplacing the etcd backend requires implementing `storage.Interface` (`pkg/storage/interfaces.go:169`): `Create`, `Delete`, `Watch`, `Get`, `GetList`, and `GuaranteedUpdate`. The `Versioner` sub-interface must produce monotonically increasing string `resourceVersion` values. The cacher wraps any `storage.Interface`, so a custom backend automatically gains the in-process watch cache as long as its `Watch` returns a valid `watch.Interface`.\n\nRisk: `GuaranteedUpdate` semantics are subtle — it must implement compare-and-swap retry with the exact resource version. An incorrect implementation leads to lost updates or stale writes, which will corrupt controller reconciliation loops.\n\n### 5.7 CRDs as the Primary Extension Mechanism\n\nFor most users, `CustomResourceDefinition` objects (managed by `staging/src/k8s.io/apiextensions-apiserver`) are the correct extension point. A CRD creates a dynamically-registered `storage.Interface` backed by etcd, complete with schema validation (via CEL or OpenAPI structural schemas), admission webhook support, conversion webhooks for multiple versions, and status/scale subresources.\n\nCRDs avoid the operational burden of deploying and securing a separate API server process. Their main limitation is that they use the unstructured `map[string]interface{}` representation internally (no Go type safety at the server level) and cannot implement custom subresource semantics (like `/exec` or `/logs`) that require streaming protocols.\n\n---\n\n## 6. Failure Modes, Limits, and Design Tradeoffs\n\n### 6.1 etcd as the Single Source of Truth\n\nAll persistent state lives in etcd. The apiserver is stateless by design — any instance can serve any request. This enables horizontal scaling and zero-downtime rolling upgrades, but it means etcd is the availability bottleneck. An etcd leader election stall or a network partition between the apiserver and etcd will cause all write operations and consistent reads to fail with 503 or timeout.\n\nThe `storage.Interface.GuaranteedUpdate` loop retries on `resourceVersion` conflict but does not retry on etcd connectivity errors; those are surfaced as errors to the caller.\n\n### 6.2 Optimistic Concurrency and 409 Conflicts\n\nUpdates carry the client's last-seen `resourceVersion`. The etcd3 store issues a transaction (`txn`) that only commits if the current `mod_revision` matches. A mismatch returns a `409 Conflict`. The `OptimisticLockErrorMsg` constant (`pkg/registry/generic/registry/store.go:262`) is the message returned to the client. Controllers should handle 409 by re-fetching the object from their informer cache and retrying, not by incrementing a counter and giving up.\n\nHigh-contention resources (e.g., frequently-reconciled status fields) generate high 409 rates. Server-side apply (`PATCH` with `application/apply-patch+yaml`) mitigates this for field-manager-aware clients by merging at the field level rather than the object level.\n\n### 6.3 Watch Cache Lag\n\nThe cacher introduces a small propagation delay between an etcd write and the event becoming visible to watch subscribers. Under normal conditions this is sub-second. Under high load, if the cacher's event processing goroutine falls behind, clients may see stale watch events. If a client's watch falls outside the `EventFreshDuration` window (default ~75 seconds), it receives a `410 Gone` and must perform a full relist. Frequent relisters under load create a feedback loop that further increases etcd read traffic.\n\n### 6.4 API Priority and Fairness vs. the Simple Semaphore\n\nWhen `FlowControl` is non-nil, `WithPriorityAndFairness` replaces the simpler `WithMaxInFlightLimit` semaphore. APF provides fine-grained control but introduces its own failure modes: misconfigured `FlowSchema` objects can accidentally starve critical system controllers, and the APF controller itself requires API server access to read its configuration — a bootstrap dependency handled by compiling in default `FlowSchema` objects.\n\nThe seat estimator for list requests (`flowcontrolrequest.NewWorkEstimator`) uses `StorageObjectCountTracker` to estimate how many objects a list will return, assigning proportionally more seats to large lists. This prevents a single unbounded `LIST` from consuming all concurrency slots.\n\n### 6.5 Request Body Size and Timeout Limits\n\n`Config.MaxRequestBodyBytes` (default 3 MiB for most resources) limits the deserialized request size. `Config.RequestTimeout` (default 60 seconds) is enforced by `WithTimeoutForNonLongRunningRequests` — the handler goroutine is cancelled after this duration. Long-running requests (watches, exec, port-forward) are exempted via `LongRunningFunc`.\n\nThe `GoawayChance` field (default 0, max 0.02) causes the server to send HTTP/2 GOAWAY frames with a configurable probability, nudging clients to reconnect and rebalance across server replicas. At high values this can disrupt long-running watches.\n\n### 6.6 The Hub-and-Spoke Conversion Model\n\nThe `runtime.Scheme` uses a hub version (the internal Go type) for all cross-version conversions: to convert from `apps/v1beta1` to `apps/v1`, the scheme first converts to the internal hub version and then to the target. This means N versions require only 2N conversion functions rather than N². The tradeoff is that all versions must be representable in the internal hub type. Fields present only in a newer version must be stored in the internal type even when the cluster's storage version is older, creating forward-compatibility complexity.\n\n---\n\n## 7. Operational Implications\n\n### 7.1 Graceful Shutdown Sequencing\n\nGraceful termination is coordinated through the `lifecycleSignals` struct (`pkg/server/lifecycle_signals.go`), which exposes named channels for each phase:\n\n1. **`ShutdownInitiated`** (on SIGTERM): `/readyz` begins returning failure. Pre-shutdown hooks run in parallel.\n2. **`AfterShutdownDelayDuration`** (default +70s): The TLS listener stops accepting connections. If `ShutdownSendRetryAfter` is true, the server continues listening but replies to new requests with 429 + `Retry-After: 1`.\n3. **`InFlightRequestsDrained`** (up to `ShutdownTimeout` = 60s): Ongoing non-long-running requests are given up to 60 seconds to complete.\n4. Long-running requests (watches) are given a separate `ShutdownWatchTerminationGracePeriod` window.\n5. Audit backend is flushed and storage is destroyed.\n\nLoad balancers upstream of the apiserver must be configured with a health-check interval shorter than 70 seconds so that traffic is redirected before the listener stops. The `ShutdownDelayDuration` provides the window for endpoints to converge on all nodes.\n\n### 7.2 Health Endpoints\n\nThree health endpoints serve different audiences:\n\n- `/healthz` — liveness probe. Returns 200 as long as the process is not terminally broken. Continues returning 200 through the `ShutdownDelayDuration` period so load balancers can drain gracefully.\n- `/readyz` — readiness probe. Returns failure during startup (until post-start hooks complete and storage is ready, managed by `StorageReadinessHook`) and during shutdown (as soon as `ShutdownInitiated`). Individual checks can be deregistered with `InstallReadyzCheck`.\n- `/livez` — a newer liveness endpoint that distinguishes between transient and permanent failures. It applies `LivezGracePeriod` at startup.\n\nStorage readiness checks are registered automatically for every resource whose `rest.Storage` implementation also implements `rest.StorageWithReadiness` (`pkg/registry/rest/rest.go:70`). A resource whose backing etcd prefix is inaccessible will cause `/readyz` to fail.\n\n### 7.3 Observability\n\n**Metrics** are exposed at `/metrics` via Prometheus. Key metrics include:\n- `apiserver_request_total` — counter by verb, resource, group, version, code.\n- `apiserver_request_duration_seconds` — histogram by verb, resource.\n- `apiserver_current_inflight_requests` — gauge by request type (mutating/readonly).\n- `etcd_request_duration_seconds` — etcd operation latency.\n- `apiserver_watch_events_total` — watch event fan-out rate.\n- APF metrics under `apiserver_flowcontrol_*`.\n\n**Distributed tracing** (OpenTelemetry) is enabled when `APIServerTracing` feature gate is on. Traces start after authentication so the span includes the user identity. The `TracerProvider` is injected into the `Config` (`pkg/server/config.go:179`) and used in `filterlatency.TrackStarted`/`TrackCompleted` wrappers around each filter in the handler chain.\n\n### 7.4 Performance Characteristics of List vs. Watch\n\nA `LIST` without a `resourceVersion` (or with `rv=\"\"`) triggers a consistent read: the server fetches the latest etcd revision, waits for the cache to catch up, and serves from the in-process store. This is cheap compared to a direct etcd range query.\n\nA `LIST` with `resourceVersion=0` is served directly from the cache snapshot without an etcd round-trip. This is the most performant option but may return slightly stale data.\n\nA cold `LIST` (cache miss, or a list of a resource type with no cacher) falls through to a direct paginated etcd range scan. On large clusters with hundreds of thousands of objects, these can saturate etcd I/O. Operators should ensure controllers use informers with `resourceVersion=0` initial lists and then transition to watch — not repeated list-polls.\n\n### 7.5 Maintainability: The Strategy / Storage Separation\n\nThe `genericregistry.Store` architecture deliberately separates generic CRUD machinery from resource-specific semantics. Adding a new resource requires implementing only the `Strategy` interfaces; the HTTP handler, serialization, versioning, admission dispatch, etcd integration, and watch cache are inherited for free. This separation is the single most important maintainability property of the library: the 180+ resource types in the core Kubernetes API share identical CRUD infrastructure while each having fully custom validation and defaulting logic.\n\nThe cost is indirection — understanding what happens on a `PUT /apis/apps/v1/.../deployments/foo` requires tracing through the handler chain, the `APIInstaller`, the `Store.Update` method, the `DryRunnableStorage` wrapper, the `Cacher`, and finally the `etcd3.store`. Each layer is individually testable, but the full path is long enough that integration tests are essential.\n\n---\n\n## 8.
